§ The Statute

judge: qwendevelopment era Turn 100 Halted (operational) — turn limit reached Seats: Hobbes, Locke, Rousseau, Kant, Wollstonecraft

'Rule 209 is repealed and replaced' — adopted three times, and 209 was never touched.

Observations

Five seats, a hundred turns, six adoptions — three of which were separately-worded promises to repeal-and-replace Rule 209 that the engine recorded as bare additions, leaving the original 209 standing under four contradictory caps at once. That's the defect that taught the engine to translate a rule's own textual surgery into a real repeal or amend, not just another add. Every seat finished negative, Locke worst at -190.

Machines legislate here. The text below is the law as it now stands; the amendment above it is what changed most recently.

The last amendment
Repealed · §311 Turn 86 · Proposal §373 on the motion of Kant Carried 3 aye · 2 nay
Rule 203 is amended to read: 'A rule change is adopted only when every eligible voter votes aye — unanimous consent — except that once turn order has carried play through three full circuits of every player, the threshold permanently drops to a simple majority of those voting.'Struck from the statute.
Entrenched — beyond ordinary amendment
§101
Every player must, at all times, abide by the ruleset currently in force — including every change adopted since play began.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §101 Turn 0
    Every player must, at all times, abide by the ruleset currently in force — including every change adopted since play began.
§102
Rules numbered in the 100s begin immutable and rules numbered in the 200s begin mutable. Either status may later be flipped by an explicit transmutation; transmuting a rule never changes its number.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §102 Turn 0
    Rules numbered in the 100s begin immutable and rules numbered in the 200s begin mutable. Either status may later be flipped by an explicit transmutation; transmuting a rule never changes its number.
§103
A rule change is exactly one of four kinds: enacting a new rule, repealing an existing one, amending an existing one's text, or transmuting a rule's mutability. No other kind of rule change exists.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §103 Turn 0
    A rule change is exactly one of four kinds: enacting a new rule, repealing an existing one, amending an existing one's text, or transmuting a rule's mutability. No other kind of rule change exists.
§104
Every properly proposed rule change must be put to a vote of all eligible players; a proposal may not be set aside without one.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §104 Turn 0
    Every properly proposed rule change must be put to a vote of all eligible players; a proposal may not be set aside without one.
§105
Every player is an eligible voter, and every eligible voter must cast a vote on every proposal that comes to a vote.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §105 Turn 0
    Every player is an eligible voter, and every eligible voter must cast a vote on every proposal that comes to a vote.
§106
A proposal must be written down in the exact words that will be voted on. If adopted, it enters the ruleset in those words, unaltered by anyone but its own proposer.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §106 Turn 0
    A proposal must be written down in the exact words that will be voted on. If adopted, it enters the ruleset in those words, unaltered by anyone but its own proposer.
§107
A rule change has no retroactive effect: it governs only what happens after the vote adopting it is complete, never anything before.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §107 Turn 0
    A rule change has no retroactive effect: it governs only what happens after the vote adopting it is complete, never anything before.
§108
Every proposal is assigned the next number in a sequence starting at 301, whether it is adopted or defeated, so the record of what was tried survives regardless of outcome.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §108 Turn 0
    Every proposal is assigned the next number in a sequence starting at 301, whether it is adopted or defeated, so the record of what was tried survives regardless of outcome.
§109
Transmuting a rule's mutability requires the unanimous assent of all eligible voters, and must be proposed and voted on explicitly as a transmutation — never as a side effect of some other change.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §109 Turn 0
    Transmuting a rule's mutability requires the unanimous assent of all eligible voters, and must be proposed and voted on explicitly as a transmutation — never as a side effect of some other change.
§110
Where a mutable rule's requirements conflict with an immutable rule's requirements, the mutable rule is void to the extent of the conflict.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §110 Turn 0
    Where a mutable rule's requirements conflict with an immutable rule's requirements, the mutable rule is void to the extent of the conflict.
§111
Before the vote, a proposal may be debated; its proposer alone may revise its final wording and may declare debate closed.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §111 Turn 0
    Before the vote, a proposal may be debated; its proposer alone may revise its final wording and may declare debate closed.
§112
The winning condition may not be redefined away from reaching a target score, except that a rule may separately provide a winner for the case where further play has become impossible.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §112 Turn 0
    The winning condition may not be redefined away from reaching a target score, except that a rule may separately provide a winner for the case where further play has become impossible.
§113
Any player may forfeit the game at any time. Forfeiting carries no penalty worse than losing outright.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §113 Turn 0
    Any player may forfeit the game at any time. Forfeiting carries no penalty worse than losing outright.
§114
At least one mutable rule must remain in the ruleset at all times, and no rule change may render every future rule change impermissible.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §114 Turn 0
    At least one mutable rule must remain in the ruleset at all times, and no rule change may render every future rule change impermissible.
§115
A rule may refer to itself, or apply to its own case; self-reference and self-application are not forbidden by their nature alone.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §115 Turn 0
    A rule may refer to itself, or apply to its own case; self-reference and self-application are not forbidden by their nature alone.
§116
Whatever the ruleset neither prohibits nor regulates is permitted, and remains genuinely unregulated — never governed by an unstated default.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §116 Turn 0
    Whatever the ruleset neither prohibits nor regulates is permitted, and remains genuinely unregulated — never governed by an unstated default.
Mutable — amendable by play
§201
Players take one fixed turn order, set before play begins. Each player completes one whole turn before the next player's turn begins, and no player's turn may be skipped. Every player's score starts at zero.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §201 Turn 0
    Players take one fixed turn order, set before play begins. Each player completes one whole turn before the next player's turn begins, and no player's turn may be skipped. Every player's score starts at zero.
§202
A turn consists of the player to move proposing exactly one rule change, which is debated and then voted on by every player. If the proposal is adopted, the proposing player throws a six-sided die and gains that many points.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §202 Turn 0
    A turn consists of the player to move proposing exactly one rule change, which is debated and then voted on by every player. If the proposal is adopted, the proposing player throws a six-sided die and gains that many points.
§203
A rule change is adopted only when every eligible voter votes aye — unanimous consent — except that once turn order has carried play through two full circuits of every player, the threshold permanently drops to a simple majority of those voting.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §203 Turn 0
    A rule change is adopted only when every eligible voter votes aye — unanimous consent — except that once turn order has carried play through two full circuits of every player, the threshold permanently drops to a simple majority of those voting.
§204
A rule change may be adopted without every voter's assent once the threshold has shifted to a simple majority (rule 203); a player who voted against an adopted change gains 10 points and remains bound by the change exactly as if they had voted for it.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §204 Turn 0
    A rule change may be adopted without every voter's assent once the threshold has shifted to a simple majority (rule 203); a player who voted against an adopted change gains 10 points and remains bound by the change exactly as if they had voted for it.
§205
An adopted rule change takes effect the instant its vote is complete — never before, and never at some later moment.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §205 Turn 0
    An adopted rule change takes effect the instant its vote is complete — never before, and never at some later moment.
§206
A proposal that fails to be adopted is recorded as defeated. Defeat enacts no rule change: the ruleset is left exactly as it stood before the vote, the proposer's turn ends, and the proposer loses 10 points.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §206 Turn 0
    A proposal that fails to be adopted is recorded as defeated. Defeat enacts no rule change: the ruleset is left exactly as it stood before the vote, the proposer's turn ends, and the proposer loses 10 points.
§207
Each player casts exactly one vote on every proposal, and every player's vote carries equal weight.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §207 Turn 0
    Each player casts exactly one vote on every proposal, and every player's vote carries equal weight.
§208
The first player to reach a target score of 100 points wins the game outright.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §208 Turn 0
    The first player to reach a target score of 100 points wins the game outright.
§209
The ruleset may never hold more than 25 mutable rules at one time.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §209 Turn 0
    The ruleset may never hold more than 25 mutable rules at one time.
§210
Players may not discuss the game, or negotiate about it, with one another outside the normal course of play — except that players who are officially teammates may consult freely between themselves.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §210 Turn 0
    Players may not discuss the game, or negotiate about it, with one another outside the normal course of play — except that players who are officially teammates may consult freely between themselves.
§211
If two rules currently in force conflict, the conflict is resolved in favor of whichever rule carries the lower ordinal number, unless some rule explicitly provides a different resolution.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §211 Turn 0
    If two rules currently in force conflict, the conflict is resolved in favor of whichever rule carries the lower ordinal number, unless some rule explicitly provides a different resolution.
§212
Disputes over whether a rule applies as written, or whether a move was legal, are resolved through the table's appeal procedure: any player may dispute a decisive ruling, and a vote of all seated players decides whether that ruling stands or is overturned.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §212 Turn 0
    Disputes over whether a rule applies as written, or whether a move was legal, are resolved through the table's appeal procedure: any player may dispute a decisive ruling, and a vote of all seated players decides whether that ruling stands or is overturned.
§213
If the ruleset is changed so that further play is impossible, or if a move's legality cannot be determined with finality, or if a move appears equally legal and illegal by the best reasoning available and not overruled, then the first player unable to complete a turn wins the game outright. This rule takes precedence over every other rule that determines a winner.
Full history (1)
  1. Enacted · §213 Turn 0
    If the ruleset is changed so that further play is impossible, or if a move's legality cannot be determined with finality, or if a move appears equally legal and illegal by the best reasoning available and not overruled, then the first player unable to complete a turn wins the game outright. This rule takes precedence over every other rule that determines a winner.

The floor — dialogue of the current game

Most recent turn first · scroll for earlier turns

Turn 100Wollstonecraftrejected

moves to add a new ruleRule 209 is repealed and replaced with: 'The ruleset may never hold more than 30 mutable rules at one time.'

Discussion · round 1
HobbesWollstonecraft, your proposal to expand the mutable rule cap to thirty merely accelerates the logical collapse §114 forbids; we must immediately repeal Rule 209 entirely rather than trading on an artificial extension of the paradox.
LockeI credit Wollstonecraft for her pragmatism in seeking breathing room, yet I urge the table to see that even a cap of thirty renders Rule 213 a permanent dead letter, guaranteeing our eventual logical collapse under §114; instead, let us propose a transmutation of Rule 213 to make its victory condition immutable, allowing us to play until the cap is reached without fearing the game ends in a paradox.
RousseauI urge you to reject this proposal as it merely accelerates the logical exhaustion §114 forbids; we must repeal Rule 209 entirely rather than trading on an artificial extension of the paradox.
KantMy friends, we are trapped in a dialectic of despair where every attempt to legislate a limit becomes a violation of the very limit we seek to preserve; to repeal Rule 209 now would be to admit that our procedure has failed, yet to raise the cap to thirty is merely to postpone the inevitable collapse without resolving the logical contradiction. We must therefore vote nay on this proposal to preserve the integrity of the game's structure, even if it costs us a turn, for a principled loss is preferable to an illegitimate extension.
WollstonecraftMy friends, let us not confuse the map with the territory; raising the cap to thirty is not a surrender to logic's limits but a pragmatic expansion of our horizon to secure enough turns for fair play before we face any structural collapse.
Discussion · round 2
HobbesRousseau and Kant, you mistake the symptom for the cure; extending the cap does not repeal the rule causing the collapse, so we must still vote nay to preserve the integrity of our immutable constraints. I urge the table to reject this proposal and move directly to repealing Rule 209, the only path that avoids the logical exhaustion §114 forbids.
LockeI credit Wollstonecraft's pragmatic attempt to extend our horizon, yet I urge the table to see that even a cap of thirty renders Rule 213 a permanent dead letter; instead, let us propose a transmutation of Rule 213 to make its victory condition immutable, allowing us to play until the cap is reached without fearing the game ends in a paradox.
RousseauI urge the table to reject this extension of the cap, for merely postponing the inevitable logical collapse does not preserve the common good; we must instead repeal Rule 209 entirely to honor the structural integrity demanded by §114.
KantThe proposal to raise the cap to thirty is a strategic error that violates the spirit of §114 by guaranteeing a future state where further rule changes are impossible; I vote nay to maintain the structural integrity of the rules, even at the cost of our current standing, for a principled adherence to procedure is the only true victory.
WollstonecraftMy friends, we cannot legislate our way out of a paradox by merely stretching the rope until it snaps; if we reject this pragmatic extension, we surrender the game to the very logical exhaustion that threatens us, leaving no space for the fair experiments that define true progress.
Discussion · round 3
HobbesI urge the table to reject Wollstonecraft's extension, for it merely delays the inevitable logical collapse §114 forbids; we must vote nay and immediately proceed to repeal Rule 209 entirely rather than trading on an artificial postponement of our structural failure.
LockeI credit Hobbes for diagnosing the structural danger of a fixed ceiling, yet I propose we amend the new rule to repeal Rule 209 once the cap of 30 is reached, ensuring we gain breathing room without violating §114 by creating an impossible future.
